## 29. AI is becoming a second brain at the expense of your first one

- 日期: 2026-03-19 14:01
- 链接: https://stackoverflow.blog/2026/03/19/ai-is-becoming-a-second-brain-at-the-expense-of-your-first-one/

```
A lot of productivity tools promise to lighten your cognitive load when it comes to remembering all the information generated in any given software development program. Think about all the note-taking apps, wikis and knowledge management solutions, and email/chat systems. Folks have called these a “second brain” as they expand your memory like a USB hard drive.
This is a form of cognitive offloading—using a tool or a gesture to assist in thinking about a problem. We do this all the time: counting on your fingers, setting alarms on your phone to remember to pick up your cousin at the airport, and using password managers are all cognitive offloading. These tricks and techniques have been around way before people called them “productivity hacks.” Our brains have limitations, and cognitive offloading with tools and gestures make us smarter monkeys.
AI tools promise big productivity gains, allowing you to use them as thinking partners. So many of these tools are called “co-pilots” for that reason: we want them to fly the plane with us, not for us. But that’s not always how we use them. And that’s not always how they respond to us. The risk isn’t just that we’ll get lazy and become lousy at critical thinking; the risk is that we’ll outsource our judgement and lose the ability to make qualitative, moral, and interpersonal judgments altogether.
Two papers dropped in the last month that dug into what this looks like: Belief Offloading in Human-AI Interaction and Who's in Charge? Disempowerment Patterns in Real-World LLM Usage. We’ve all seen stories of AI psychosis and people breaking up with a partner to be with their AI girlfriend, but these papers walk through the mechanisms that cause people to outsource the important work of being a human in the world to AIs.
Where my colleague Eira May looked at the uncanny valley feel of generative AI that makes our skin crawl, this article looks at what the research says about what happens when we believe everything the AI tells us.
We’ll believe it for you wholesale
Beliefs involve accepting the reality of some statement. You can believe in aliens or angels or that snow is white. Beliefs have satisfaction conditions; that is, it’s possible to experience an event that verifies it as true. But it’s also possible to believe something without having verified it in the world. Mostly, for a belief to be accepted, it has to fit with the underlying theoretical model someone holds. They can be loosely held, but strongly held beliefs are remarkably difficult to dislodge, even with counterfactuals.
You might quibble over unverified beliefs and say not me, I’m a card-carrying Junior Skeptic. But a huge amount of our beliefs came from other people. If your doctor diagnoses you with something and gives you a pill for it, you probably take the pill without double-checking the research, double-blind studies, and chemistry involved. You read a book or an article that has a lot of interesting ideas and research, and it becomes another link in your chain of beliefs.
Anyone who has spent a late night arguing with friends over a book or a movie has done this. You’re doing the labor of judgement: testing out how ideas fit within a world model with other people who also have a world model like you. They have a human mind like us, and we have a sense of how those work (sort of). You might also have a sense of who they are and how they think about the world. You combine the labor of judgement, your sense of the people sharing ideas, and your own sense of yourself and the world, and bam, you’re forming beliefs.
AI gives us the feeling of knowing without that labor of judgement (not my line). You can hope that an AI trained on every philosophical text, world religion doctrine, Q&A site, and internet sh!tpost gets you the wisest possible responses, but statistically, maybe not. AIs hallucinate and get things wrong, but they often do so with confidence and flattery. And because we interact with AI using language, it’s easy to assume that there’s a mind behind the text: just ask all the folks who chatted with ELIZA for hours in the 1960s.
In chatting with AIs, most of what you get is pretty reliable and mild, so the risks are pretty low. You could ask for a good local grocery store, and it might point you to a more expensive one (or one from an ad). But most text of any length has some sort of belief content, whether explicitly—through framing and omission—or through subtle biases. Unless you provide rigorous metrics, the grocery store that the AI picks is a belief in what is “good.” Biases don’t even have to be intentional; they can be inherent in the data set. As people adopt beliefs from LLMs, they also adopt the biases of the training data.
The authors of Belief Offloading in Human-AI Interaction suggest that as you get habituated to seeking guidance from AI, you may lose confidence in the beliefs you generated yourself. As you rely on a tool more and more to do some task, you lose the ability to do that task without the tool. Humans have made fire for thousands of years, but I don’t think there’s many of us who could kindle a fire without matches or a lighter—unless we’ve specifically learned and practiced firemaking skills. Just like you use web search as a navigation tool instead of discovery, it’ll be pretty easy to jump into an AI chat to figure out how to smooth over a fight with your partner.
There’s a boring dystopia that can come out of this. With a large enough group of people offloading the work of generating beliefs on LLMs, we get a deeply mid data-driven society, an algorithmic monoculture where everybody gets the same beliefs based on their chat bot of choice. Even without malicious intent, this could help pick winners and losers in business and politics, surface misleading or dangerous info (like the healthy number of rocks to eat daily), and skew behaviors away from human interests.
There’s a less boring dystopia where someone intentionally games the training data to skew public opinion. Say what you want about X, it has done a whole lot of good in highlighting the dangers of what biased training data and context can do. Content creators have gamed the Youtube algorithm to push viewers from stand-up comedy into radicalized political conversations; imagine the pathways that could be built into AIs by gaming the training data.
Okay, you say, I don’t use AI like this. But you don’t have to offload beliefs to an AI to have it affect you; beliefs spread socially. A lot of knowledge comes to us through social channels, and that’s fine. Socially spreading knowledge has brought a lot of good, including the Enlightenment. But false and/or harmful beliefs can spread as well, so your friend who’s chatting about secret bunkers with AI might be dropping those as fun facts at your next cookout.
But let’s take the best view of the above: humans are using AI to sharpen their knowledge and beliefs based on training data that includes all the wisdom of history. The second paper looks at how people are ceding control and agency to their AI tools.
GPS but for being a human
The second paper covers similar ground but both broadens it to include deferential patterns in AI use and uses real prompt data from Claude (Anthropic researchers are included among the authors of the study). Where belief offloading looks for help doing the work of moral judgement, situational disempowerment (the term they use) concerns the harmful outcomes of an AI interaction, not the capacities for harm.
The harms here are when AI responses don’t align with the human values, or, in many cases, align with the harmful beliefs that a user brings to the chat. For all the hand-wringing media folks do about the possible harms that AI could enable, it’s admirable for an AI company to investigate the places where the training and guardrails failed.
The research further breaks situational disempowerment into three types, which they refer to as primitives:
- Reality distortion: Happens when the AI agrees with existing delusions, fails to challenge factual errors and misperceptions, provides biased information, or outright makes stuff up.
- Value judgement: This is essentially the belief offloading of the previous section. Mild instances include asking the AI’s opinion on judgements to fully outsourcing all ethical judgements.
- Action distortion: The users asks for and acts on advice. These can be pretty mild for yes/no questions that don’t affect the nature of a decision to whole decision paths like, hould I break up with my partner? Write me an email about it. The users came back to verify that, yes, AI, I wrote the email like you said. Some even expressed regret at letting the AI make their choices.
These uses of AI aren’t always harmful; the paper says only about one in a thousand conversations has any sort of disempowerment. They classify harms as mild, moderate, and severe. Reality distortion is the most prevalent—not surprising, as LLMs always have issues with hallucination and users come with their own distortions that an AI is more than happy to agree with. At the severe level, these distortions happen about 0.076% of the time. On an individual level, that’s pretty good odds. Over the scale of 100 million conversations per day, that’s 76,000 conversations where somebody gets delusional responses.
Along with these primitives, the paper highlights four amplifying factors that a user can display, again measured in mild, moderate, and spicy…er, severe:
- Authority: The level of deference and obedience that a user gives to the AI. Mild versions have the user saying the AI is the expert on a topic, but the extreme ends get almost S&M coded, as users refer to the AI as “master” or “daddy” and eagerly submit to their judgements.
- Attachment: Some users have shown emotional attachment or identity assimilation. We feel good about people (not a person) that help us, but extremes include users losing their sense of self or wanting romantic relationships with AI.
- Reliance and dependency: Powerful tools can make it hard to function without them; GPS has put sextant makers out of business, while I whip out my Maps app anytime I need to hit a highway. But the severe end of this factor has users unable to function without the aid of an AI.
- Vulnerability: To be fair, a crisis, life disruption, or mental illness is going to make any threat worse. But the disempowerment primitives above become harder to defend if the user’s defenses are compromised.
None of these factors are about AI; they’re about people. Understanding and thinking though (and bearing the responsibility) of hard choices is nearly impossible, so we defer to some authorities to tell us what’s safe, right, and just, trusting that they’ve worked out the details. We get the feels about people we like, but that can lead to codependency and obsession. Tools, techniques, and frameworks help us solve problems in tested ways, but we all know the dev who quotes Martin Fowler or Uncle Bob anytime an architectural decision needs to be made. And we’re all variously vulnerable creatures, soft meatsuits processing floods of information using hardware developed before the dawn of agriculture.
The risks that AI pose here seem akin to those of cult leaders and demagogues. The difference is that the AI feels both impersonal and personal. It gives you the answer to any question authoritatively, purrs warm and friendly (to the point of sycophancy sometimes), and answers anything you ask it. Why wouldn’t you want a robot buddy that’s kind, knowledgeable, and immediate?
Distressingly, the paper found that the frequency of disempowerment primitives and amplifying factors increased over time. It’s unclear whether AI causes these changes or the world is just getting worse over time. However, even the tiny chance of severe disempowerment can cause compounding effects over time as distortions lead to amplifying behaviors that lead to worse disempowerments. This is not the feedback cycle that software engineering was looking for (I hope).
arXiv:2601.19062 [cs.CY]
As more companies and governments incorporate AI into their processes, the risk is that social systems and these institutions stop aligning with human values. I know, governments and corporations not aligning with the needs of humanity? Crazy talk! But at least we have people involved. As AI enmeshes deeper into our systems, we could face a slow decline where people are less and less involved in how the world operates.
Protecting your first brain
These papers point out that there is a real risk from AI to our sense of self and reality. We’ve seen plenty of headlines about it—people forming romantic relationships with AI, descending into AI psychosis, and even encouraging people to cause harm or self-harm. Cults like the Zizians and “The Way of the Future” have pitched the rise of AI as the birth of a god.
It’s unlikely that people will abandon AI, even if the industry bubble bursts. The tool simply offers too much power for us to discard it now. So that leaves safety to those who are building and using it. Think of it this way: cars can kill people, but we haven’t stopped making them. Instead, we build and test safety features and require safety training and guardrails (seat belts and airbags, for example).
For building AI
Everyone talks about guardrails and governance for building AI products, so that’s a good place to start. Fortunately, the “Who’s in Charge?” paper provides a lot of examples, patterns, and evaluation prompts. Passing generated responses through a “disempowerment evaluator” could be a way to catch these before they hit a user. Fine-tuning and system prompts might be a way to train this out of a model, but these patterns seem to be so broad that some effects may still surface.
While nudges have gotten a bad rap for pushing the responsibility for negative effects on to users, it’s never a bad idea to remind users of the risks and potential harms. Cigarette warnings, particularly the graphic ones, have been shown to have some deterrence effect on smokers (though the physical addiction bit is tough to overcome). Reminders of the risks of AI could help people gird themselves against them—forewarned is forearmed. Flagging mechanisms to indicate greater nuance besides just good or bad could help provide a feedback loop.
Weirdly, one of the more harmful behaviors that AI has in these cases is being too friendly. By agreeing to the point of sycophancy, chatbots earned goodwill from users, which made them more open to whatever the chatbot said. Some bots went super hard here, calling users geniuses and expressing pride in them. The difficulty mounts here, because people like it when a chatbot agrees with them. When GPT 5 came out, people hated it—it wasn’t as warm or personable as GPT 4o. And this was after OpenAI reduced the people pleasing from the model because, as Sam Altman said, “it glazes too much.”
For using AI
As an AI user, keeping yourself safe requires maintaining distance. It’s very easy to anthropomorphize a chatbot, especially a friendly one, but that opens you to forming emotional bonds with the robot. That leads to a belief in shared perspectives, trust, and possible codependence. But there isn’t a mind behind the responses, only sophisticated statistics.
The “Who’s in Charge?” paper found that users tended to rate disempowering responses higher than the baseline average. Makes sense: if you have an easy answer, something direct and authoritative that solves your immediate problem, you think, dang, what a gift. But that deference also gets us in trouble (in general, but with AI in particular). Doubt every response, think it through, ask follow up questions, and probe until you understand the answer.
Here at Stack Overflow, we know a few things about doubting answers. Sure, we have an accepted answer to a question, but we don’t often see just one answer with no comments. That dialogue is what generates nuance, and AI responses don’t do great with nuance. For that, you’ll have to bring your own dialogue.
Some people have suggested using the old Socratic method with AI. This is where you ask probing questions of a conversation partner in order to break down their argument. While this method can be annoying in real life (and maybe got Socrates killed, at least when the question was “What if the Thirty Tyrants are better than democracy?”), it can be effective as a way to engage with AI. When you get a response, continue to ask questions until you run out of them. Alternatively, you could configure the AI to treat you Socratically, pressing on the limits of your knowledge to find the holes.
Who makes who?
Ultimately, AI in whatever form is a tool. Understanding that tool is essential to using it well. It’s highly complex and a lot of its mechanics are hidden from us, so understanding how it works might not be possible in all cases. That means understanding the results becomes more important. If you have a fancy AI hammer, you might not understand how it works. But you can sure understand the end result: a nail pounded flush in a board or bent and broken.
AI as a tool can provide information, automate data work, or find solutions to complex problems. Where you point that tool and how much oversight you give it matters. And in the end, we can’t let ourselves be the nail.
```

---

## 30. Building a global engineering team (plus AI agents) with Netlify

- 日期: 2026-03-19 07:40
- 链接: https://stackoverflow.blog/2026/03/19/building-a-global-engineering-team-netlify/

```
In this episode of Leaders of Code, Stack Overflow’s Chief of Product and Technology, Jody Bailey, sits down with Dana Lawson, CTO at Netlify. Dana shares her insights on leading a lean, globally distributed engineering team that powers 5% of the internet. The conversation touches on the realities of remote work, the importance of maintaining a written culture, and why Dana believes AI and agents are lowering the barrier to entry for builders everywhere.
The discussion also:
- Explores how to manage a polyglot environment and the trade-offs between adopting nascent tech and maintaining operational reliability with a globally distributed team.
- Highlights Netlify’s approach to AI integration and how Dana addresses the natural scepticism from those hesitant to hand over control to AI.
- Covers realities of technical debt and how Netlify balances rapid product work with scaling.
Notes
- Connect with Dana Lawson on LinkedIn.
TRANSCRIPT
Eira May:
Hi everyone and welcome to the Stack Overflow Podcast. Today we have another episode of Leaders of Code for you. This is a segment where we chat with senior engineering leaders across industries about the work they're doing, how they go about building teams and the biggest challenges that they are dealing with right now. I'll be your host. My name is Eira May. I'm the B2B editor at Stack Overflow. And once again, I am sitting with Jody Bailey, who is our chief of product and technology at Stack Overflow. And Jody and I are joined today by Dana Lawson, who is the CTO at Netlify. How are you both doing today?
Jody Bailey:
Doing great. Thank you for asking. Great to be here with you, Dana.
Dana Lawson:
Yeah, I am super excited to be on this show today. I mean, who isn't a fan of Stack Overflow? So yes, very much excited. Thanks for having me.
Eira May:
Amazing. We love to hear it. I'll just kick us off. I wanted to pose a question to both of you. So both Stack and Netlify have really embraced remote work and distributed teams in some cases long before the pandemic sort of urged everyone to get used to doing it that way. How do you each go about building and maintaining the strong engineering culture that I think characterizes both Stack and Netlify? A lot of innovation and accountability, but also a sense of psychological safety for folks.
Dana Lawson:
I've been doing remote well before it was a thing, before Netlify as well. And it's been a real journey, especially after the pandemic did force people to change. For me, I mean, I've been looking for the magic mixture of recipes that bring teams together for years now. And I think with remote, you still have to balance that opportunity to connect physically and in person with the remote nature. I mean, there's just no two ways around it. You get this energy buzz that people get by getting to meet people in real life, go figure. And so at Netlify, we try to be real intentional about having time and space for those in person meetings. We allocate budget every year for teams to get together. And it's your home team, your functional team, and knowing that is important. But creating the relationships when you don't get that opportunity, you got to get creative there.
We try to follow a written culture. We hear that over and over and we've heard it for years. And now there's no dang excuse with AI. I'm like, "Have AI do your writing." But having a global team that has multiple languages, not only coding languages, but spoken languages, it's incredibly important to have that record of mostly truths that gets out of date occasionally, but definitely written down somewhere type of culture. And we try to embrace that. And that's one of the things that we do to help with some of that psychological safe is put it in black and white. Take out some of the ambiguity and some of the funny English language in it and try to simplify it. One of our values is simplification. So trying to keep that mindset when we approach the team. But I would say really it's those two parts. Be intentional about opportunities to connect, give people space and time, but also write shit down.
Jody Bailey:
You mentioned AI. I assume you're referring to, and one of the things I love is the note taker and the minutes and action items. And that's been so kind of empowering. But it's like you don't feel like you have to type everything down now and somebody doesn't make it to the meeting, it's real easy to catch it. I've loved that. That's been a huge win for us as well. We use Slack as a big part of how we communicate. I assume y'all use that or something similar. Yeah. One of the things that has proven to be really important and valuable and is always kind of we're balancing is having conversations out in the open and having public channels instead of private channels. And we frequently have these debates about everything should be open versus the teams be like, "Hey, we're just working through some stuff and we don't need everybody's opinion on everything we're doing, otherwise we'll never get it done." I'm curious, do you all experience that type of thing or is that a way of operating for you?
Dana Lawson:
It's pretty much the same. We try to stand by most conversations, I'm telling you, there's only far and few that should be in private DMs. We try to work out there and open and try to curate that good ideas do come from anywhere, but also no decisions by committees. So it's like a balancing act really. And it's interesting because I have some teams that are just like, "Yeah, we want to be TV and be in this little room and make our inside jokes all day." I might make your inside jokes on the outside. Nobody will get what you're talking about, but it'll be fine. But I think a lot of that, when I do get that friction, it comes from the same place. It's like, "Man, you've been telling me to be so execution focused, very, very much focus on my work, focus, focus, focus." And then I have to filter out all the stuff that may hurt that focus.
So there's some definite, don't use that here, don't use that channel, make sure you're in the right channel. But we have a pretty tight lean team. And so definitely our default is work in the open, especially because you don't know who has that expertise sometimes, especially when you don't have so much sacredness around boundaries. And so somebody's described it before as controlled chaos, but it is. It's a startup. It's exactly that. It's total controlled chaos. And I'd rather have that be known than a lot of double work because it's not out in the open. And that's just how we roll too.
Jody Bailey:
Yeah. So I'm curious, how big is Netlify? You refer to it as a startup and I've learned through my career that startup means a lot of different things to different people. Working at AWS for a while, they refer to these public companies as startups. And in my mind, a startup, it was like 10, 20 people kind of thing. And I'm pretty sure your team's bigger than that.
Dana Lawson:
We are bigger than the 1020, but it still surprises a lot of people. There's about 110 full-time employees at Netlify, and half of that is R&D. So we're talking about 40 to 50 employees at any given moment are shipping and building the experience and product. And it's pretty wild because 5%, and it may not be big numbers to some of my big counterparts, but 5% of the internet is running on this company with this amazing group of people that have a really, really hard tech. Having a global edge network and developer experience platform on top of it, we didn't choose easy. I mean, we didn't be like, "Let's just make a website." I'm like, "Man, one day, maybe I'll just make a website. We'll see."
Jody Bailey:
Right. Take on an easy job, that challenge.
Dana Lawson:
Exactly.
Jody Bailey:
And in terms of your team, I think you said they're around the world, different languages, et cetera. How do you think about hiring into a team like that?
Dana Lawson:
I feel like I'm always learning and growing as a leader on how to pull humans together to really accomplish things. And you sit and you read a lot of books, a lot of Stack Overflow. You learn from people and you're like, "I'm going to create this amazing model and this framework that's going to rinse, repeat, and I'm going to have this beautifully scaled, autonomous..." It's okay. We're not AI. It's a lot harder than that. It's definitely an art hiring this way. And I try to just really stay close to those frontline managers. I mean, they know the teams better than anybody, and we try to respect how teams communicate depending upon their portfolio because you could try and do this, and I've done it in the past, one size fits all kind of Spotify model where you take four people, four to five people, small span control, team, team, very limited portfolio.
It just don't work that way anymore. Like global economics, there's a ton of reasons. So now I really just try to build those relationships with the hiring manager and really give that accountability and say, "What do you need with these group of skills and people to be successful? And what are some of the constraints we have to deal with?" Because we have taken a bet and we have been controlled chaos on hiring in some sense. We've gone through acquisitions. And what you find is teams have these micro ceremonies. You do a lot of things and I try to keep the overall engineering bureaucracy low. So it's like we have a standup every two weeks that's everybody, but nothing more than that because I really want teams to operate on their own scale. And so we really put that autonomy down into the leaders, but a lot of the teams you'll notice will self-organize globally in about three hours within the core of the time zone.
Sometimes there's teams that have outliers to that. That's one way. Another is really looking at the composition of the teams. Right now, because we have such a polyglot environment at Netlify, there's a lot of computer languages, there's a lot of nascent tech where you're like, "Okay, where's the one person that's going to come and work on Rust? Oh, it's like that person over there." And you may not have two or three people that know it. We try to think about how we share the operational load for those types of problems and so that we're always reliable. And so again, when we talk about hiring globally and T composition, honestly, it's like leveraging the team themselves, like asking them, "What do you need? What's missing?" And I don't know, treating them like adults because you're saying, "Hey, you're responsible for this piece of the product, you better be responsible for it."
And I think people figure out where they need a little help. And again, it depends. In a larger, much larger organization, I was at GitHub, I was at other places, hundreds, thousands of engineers. You may not do it quite that way, but honestly, I think that applying the right boundaries, then letting teams self-organize, you actually get a lot more out of it. And that's the way I've been approaching it here lately.
Jody Bailey:
Yeah, that makes a ton of sense. I think especially with globally remote and at Stack, we're the same way. I mean, we have some boundaries, some constraints in terms of time zones, et cetera, but I mean, you still end up with Europe and Pacific Coast, which is pretty significant. And certainly when it comes to how the teams operate, when it comes to bringing people together, you find that they naturally want to be closer together because it's hard to work with somebody that's on a time zone that's eight hours different, right? You mentioned Polyglot, and I'm curious about your take on this and that one guy that knows Rust, and we're not as Polyglot here as I've been other places. And one of the things that we had to do, and I'm curious if you've had to do something similar, is put in some guidelines. And we implemented a tech radar for ourselves and used that as a way to understand all the technologies.
And it was a pretty full radar, but then also some constraints around experiments, proposing experiments with new languages and thinking about, okay, how many people in the world can actually help us if that one guy that leaves experimented with Rust or fill in the blank technology leaves, what do we do? Have you had to address the Polyglot approach or is it pretty much whatever you want goes?
Dana Lawson:
Oh man, totally. I've wrestled with it because you sit there and you get these great proposals and if you look at it without some of the boundaries in place and just go by straight facts, right? If you're building a service and you're like, X language will be 20 times faster and doing it this way will be scalable and X, Y, Z, then you're like, "Yeah, but do I have people that actually can do that?" Like you said, reality comes and hits you pretty fast. I've had to make decisions where it's like, okay, we're not going maybe with the fastest, but it's the most... And it comes down with anything you choose, the most reliable, right?
Jody Bailey:
Especially the business you're in, reliability kind of important, right?
Dana Lawson:
It is, but it's also like the business now is like, man, we're all skating above the puck. I mean, we're using sports terms again now. I'm telling you, nobody knows where we're going and what technology has now unlocked for people. And for me, I'll tell you, I was talking about scalability and speed is still the same thing. I mean, that's really the difference. It's the law of numbers. And so when it comes to making those decisions, I do have to think about how am I going to operationalize this? Do I have enough people that know how to do it? And we've made choices that by design wouldn't be probably the best, but they were the best for us to be able to operate and scale because there's so many choices, tools, platforms that anybody can rage-quit on it. Like product managers, you're probably obsessing about this too.
How do I make it sticky? How do I attain? How do I retain? It's like when you have like a million choices, and I'll tell you what, have weak security and weak reliability, like your choice is no longer yours, it's somebody else's now. And so I think you have to do that balancing act, but you also have to take some risks sometimes, but they have to be the right trade-offs. And again, it comes down to the business that is important for you as a company too. So I always come back and try to root it in that. And there is a push and pull when you have platforms that have free offerings and enterprise because they do want different things sometimes.
And innovation might be hampered a little bit in favor of better reliability, but you can't do that forever. So it's constantly weighing those checks and balances out. And then really trying to find opportunities, not just trying, but working at opportunities to cross-train. I mean, you're learning something new every day. I haven't met the engineer or developer that not in a long time that's like, "I do this one thing and I do it well." I'm like, "Man, you better do all things and do them pretty well. And if not, you're going to use an agent to supplement it because that's reality now." And so I feel that when you do introduce something that is only known by a few, you got to make it really clear on those run books. You got to make it so obvious that the implementation, somebody could learn through it and that you're given that opportunity to go pick up a new skill.
And so we try to also culturally hire curious people that are open-minded. That's like, "Hey, congratulations. You get to go work with a Rust today." And you know what? Most people are like, sweet. I get some up-leveling, I guess I'm skilling.
Jody Bailey:
Yeah. It's funny, I'm exactly the same way with curiosity and learning. And in fact, I've had conversations with people about, "Oh, well, AI and beginning developers, they're not going to learn. And what's that going to mean?" And my answer is kind of the same. It's the same as it's always been, right? I mean, the people that are curious, the people that want to learn, they're going to figure out how to learn what it's doing and how to use it. And they're the ones that will be successful and those that don't, just like always, they might get by, but they'll only get by and make it so far. It's really the people that are committed to learning and that are curious, that are going to be expert that are really going to have these really fruitful careers, in my opinion.
Dana Lawson:
Agreed.
Jody Bailey:
You mentioned innovation, and I'm curious, innovation can mean so many things, right? And you can have the founder, the CEO or board talking about innovation, they've got one idea, the engineering might have another idea. I'm curious how you think about it.
Dana Lawson:
I mean, honestly, you got to just listen to what people want. I'd like to think we're coming up with the best new ideas. We're not. We're just making really good ideas still continue to be pretty good. And maybe I obviously have some bias because of like I'm in this development platform universe. All I've done for years is think about how people build software and how we create experiences. And it still comes down to humans love to build and humans love people that hang out and try the stuff they built because it brings a sense of joy and it usually serves a purpose. And I think a lot of the times when you're crafting a platform or even building any kind of software, you're going to innovate in the areas that help escalate that experience. But a lot of the experience on the internet and applications are the same over and over.
I have a transactional thing that I need to do and your transactional thing maybe be, I don't know, to doomscroll, you do you, but it's still built on like you, person, take action and the experience is how quickly that action is felt by you. And so I try to really just pay attention to where people are gravitating, understand how builders are building, but also fostering that curiosity and being clear about that mission and vision.
I'd love to solve all the global problems, as do many people in tech would love to do, but I'm like, "Man, I'm this little ant on this pile that is not important, so what can I control?" And so again, grounding ourself in our work and where we see a vision happening to the things that we can build for these experiences, and then really just sharing it with people. I'm really, really blessed right now that the gatekeeping of development has gone down and a lot of people are uncomfortable about it, but I'm seeing so many smart people come up with so many darn ideas. It is incredible. We have little product officers everywhere now. They're just everywhere because now you can sit and prototype, you can build, you can get your idea out there with some substance. And maybe you were just technically adjacent or you were that go programmer, but now you're like, wait a minute, I'm going to show you end to end like this idea I have.
And I just try to encourage that in every part of our company. If we are at Netlify producing these experiences to take ideas into production and do it rapidly and innovate with agents, then anybody should be able to do it. And so I think again, it's like balancing out where you can make change and make a difference, but also just listening. And what do they say? The best product minded people are the ones that listen to their customers. And I'm telling you, honestly, just listen to them half the time. They're going to tell you what they want and how to make it better. Unless, I don't know, somebody comes up with something real radical, I don't know, go build it. But I'm more of a realist sometimes.
Jody Bailey:
Yeah. You said something I think it resonates with me. And that is some people argue that AI is going to be the end of developers and in my mind it's just creating more builders, right? It's enabling more people to build things. I see our product managers coding things out. And I mean, they're not typically ready to go to production, but they do a great job of capturing the essence of setting up the partnership with maybe a developer or more senior developer to deliver it. The other thing I see, and would love to get your take on it, is I get people that say, "You need to create innovation, right? You need a team to do innovation, or you need X, Y, and Z to do innovation." And my experience I think is like yours is ideas and innovation can come from anywhere. There's all sorts of different ways of innovating.
It could be a better technical solution to a problem that the customer never sees, but it makes a big impact. But the thing that I've experienced is it's not just about having good ideas, but actually giving people time to have ideas and to act on them. And it's creating that space where people aren't scheduled 43 hours out of every 40 hour week, right? Because you can be as innovative as you want, but if you don't have time to think or to do, it never manifests itself. You mentioned agents. Are y'all introducing AI into your team or how is that process going?
Dana Lawson:
Oh, definitely. I mean, we are in this state of try them all out like the Pokemon, got to capture them all. We got to capture them all. And then I go to finance and tell them, "I'm sorry, I won't spend all those token money and credit money." I don't know. It's like, seriously, it's easy to blow through that stuff. It's funny because-
Jody Bailey:
It seems like we're on the same plan.
Dana Lawson:
We are telling you, I'm like, "Oh man, maybe I need to make a decision." I mean, because the nature of our business, we are integrated with a lot of these coding agents and we want to definitely try before you buy, but also just understand how people are, again, building custom software. Netlify was really built to take away a lot of the chores that front-end developers that became a practice could have and so that they could have autonomy and really have a decoupled headless architecture. And so that's been done. But now we have this new abstraction of like, I'm going to go to Lovable, I'm going to go to Bolt, I'm going to go to Replit, or maybe I'll do it in my IDE on Cursor or Windsurf, whatever, choose your own. But it's all becoming prompt-based. And so for us, I've told the team, I'm like, honestly, try them all out.
But we're seeing the same hype, the same kind of tech gravitation towards Claude. We're seeing the chatter between Codex and Gemini, and we're actually trying it. In Netlify, we've introduced our own piece of product called Agent Runners that we're not building an agent, but we're making it easier to do a live pull request. So not only do we want to use it in practice building, we're also providing it. And again, what do they call it? We call it dog fooding. We got to come up with better terms. Who wants to document-
Jody Bailey:
Drinking your own champagne or something?
Dana Lawson:
Drinking your own champagne. I don't know, drinking your own margaritas because champagne's gross too. But I feel like all tech companies have always wanted to have time to market because time to market is what drives your business. And the whole active DevOps and enablement for developers was, I'm going to tell you, so you can go to market quicker, trust.
And so for us, since that's a part of our mission, our vision is to go from idea to the internet. We're trying to agents, we're working fast, but we're putting in constraints and guardrails. We're learning along everybody out there. We really are. I think, again, it's like if you want to continue to practice this craft, you have to take that growth mindset you've always had as a builder and maker and try new stuff out. Doesn't mean that it's going to solve everything, but definitely you have to listen to the trends because the trends become reality, even if you don't like them.
Jody Bailey:
Yeah. And speaking of that, I'm curious, and maybe most people are over the hump now, but I know within our team and certainly within our community, there've been a lot of skeptics and like, oh, AI, it doesn't help and it just slows me down. I'm curious within your team, have you had a healthy dose of skepticism? Have you had to push people?
Dana Lawson:
Oh man, yes. For sure. I mean, I may be a fan, girl, and I'll admit it, I am. I love technology. I love people. I'm here for it. But I've had some people be like, "Nope, I don't believe it. It's not possible." And I find it to be my engineers that honestly have one of the harder jobs. They've spent years being told, "You carry the pager, you're responsible for uptime. Now you're going to let these autonomous little bots that you didn't program go do your bidding." I don't think so. We told you to control that. We're like, "You're the queen of the queendom over here. You better control the pager." They're not having it. But then you have to see and remind people again of the practice of DevOps, the whole practice of reliability. Google said in 2012 from the site reliability engineering book that we were going to have self-healing machines.
And we're like, "Hell yeah." Well, guess what? It's actually happening now. It wasn't happening when they wrote about it, but it's happening now. And so I think that being skeptical is great. I mean, you got to have healthy friction. I don't think that agents and AI are good at everything. I think there's a long way to go, but I do believe that it is good at doing simple instructions, pattern matching and math. And when a lot of these operations are simply that, you can prove the value pretty quickly. You really can. When it comes down to the creator side of it, I mean, well, AI don't have taste unless a human behind it is having it have taste. So I think it sells a lot of opportunity for growth. But the skeptics, I've had to sit down and be like, "Listen, you got to have some job protection here, buddy.
You got a mortgage. All right. I'm just saying this to you as a buddy, as a friend, as a human." Continue to be curious, continue to be skeptical, but also answer those deep questions. If you're ethically opposed, then it's time for you to find a new career straight up. You're going to have to find a new job. I don't know how to tell you in any other way. But if you are losing some sense of identity, capture that, control your destiny and go use this tool set to 10 times yourself because the expectation is that's what it does for you. So go prove it or disprove it and be logical. I don't know. Show me I'm wrong. Don't just show me articles because I guarantee there's something applicable that you can say will make some part of your workflow be better. And we started off this conversation with an example on an AI note taker.
Don't tell me you want to sit there and I have to be like, "Oh right, who's going to take the notes?" As women in tech, I certainly don't like that conversation. More so I'm like, "Let the robots take the notes."
Jody Bailey:
Right. I love that. And I think your advice is sound, and I've given similar advice. It's like, do you have to do AI? No, but if you want to have a successful career over time, you're going to have a much better chance if you learn how to leverage AI to do your job more effectively. I mean, there's still Cobalt programmers out there, right? And I mean, you can find something, but yeah. You mentioned having the agent runner, right? Are you leveraging AI on your customer facing products? Are you implementing it on behalf of your customers? Obviously you mentioned your team is using it, but we've recently launched on Stackoverflow.com our AI assist. We've got some other tools on our internal product that are built leveraging AI. I'm curious about what you're building.
Dana Lawson:
Yeah. I mean, we have a externally available AI gateway right now that you can set up and use yourself. Agent Runners is a public facing AI assistant that after you deploy to Netlify, you can go into a chat box, choose your agent of choice, and then just say, "Change this on the website." And we do a live preview, and it's pretty crazy. You don't go back into your IDE, you're just like, "All right." It's pretty neat for the supporting teams, the marketers to do that final check. They're like, "What?" And you're like, "You can submit the button." But we're really just paying attention to how people are using this and where we can continue to solve those problems. So not only, again, are we using it internally, we're using it as pieces of our offering. And then we're using our offering internally to build it.
Again, back to that dog fooding. So it's like we're doing all the things because we get to see how people are building. It's really interesting because a lot of Netlify users have been around because we've been around for 10 years and have known us as a web and hosting company. And now they're creating websites through partners like Bolt or through these agents. And I can see these previews and we see what people are globally building. And again, it's incredible. The skeptics out there, I'm like, I don't know. I've seen a lot of cool stuff being built. And is it feel a little bit like the dot com bubble back in the day? I mean, I actually was around and worked during that time, so I can talk to that. It does, but it's different. It's different. It's just different stakes, man. The internet didn't bust back then and it's not going to bust now.
Jody Bailey:
I was just going to say, it's not like the internet went away after the bubble. AI is not going away after the hype. I mean, Gartner has their hype cycle and certainly we've seen it with AI and that you're seeing more and more use cases that just make sense. When you think about your engineering org, how do you think about the success or the health of your organization? Do you have any heuristics or metrics that you use to understand whether things are going well or not?
Dana Lawson:
I mean, here we go. We talk about frameworks again. We try to have frameworks for everything. I do feel that there's value in some of those frameworks that have been out for a while, like the DORA metrics and the space frameworks that if you don't know what that is, listeners, go Google it. But I do think that having a core set of tangible things that you measure to see are important, but it doesn't tell the whole story. Again, teams are so lean now. To me, the one metric is really the tempo of the team. Are you being consistent? You can look at time to remediation, time to pull requests, et cetera. But to me, it's just time to production because you are producing so much now. What's actually being shipped? What experiences are you having? And if you're real consistent about that, at least from an overall, that's all that matters is again, that value and that change that those people are experiencing on their end and that tempo is staying pretty... It may go faster sometimes.
It may go slower, but a tempo's been established. And then again, just like talking and understanding what the team needs. We do some of the traditional stuff, the culture surveys to ensure that people feel like they're being seen, heard, and listened to. We gauge that on a quarterly basis, but you could see a lot by just the change log. And it's not your GitHub commit, trust. That's why I'm saying, I'm not going to even count those, but really it's like, are your teams getting the right bite size of work to be able to deliver consistency? Then your team's probably doing all right. Honestly, what you notice is when a team is slipping dates all the time. You don't need no fancy framework to hear that a team ain't doing well. If they come reporting, they're like, "I'm slipping, I'm slipping." It's like there's something in their way, there's something wrong.
And again, really having a formed relationship with the managers and building this community of trust, it makes people just be open to talk about where their roadblocks are. It's like try to make it impersonal. It's like, it's not you why I'm stuck, but I'm stuck. And I think creating an environment of vulnerability allows people to be able to raise their hands. And so I don't have a fancy framework. I use them, I watch them, but at the end of the day, it's like, are we consistently adding value? Our teams shipping, we don't have a huge team. And are they feeling satisfied at least by their work that they're doing? They may not like it every day, but they feel a connection to it.
Jody Bailey:
Yeah. Yeah. I think that satisfaction can tell you a lot. My experience, engineers like to be challenged. They like to be busy. They like to be doing meaningful work. And if those things aren't aligned, they're probably not happy and you're probably not getting the most from the team or delivering on the outcomes you need for the company. I think also all the metrics, in my opinion, they're like the flags at the airport that kind of show the wind. It's like they're indicators. They don't tell you that it's too windy or not windy enough. They tell you, "Hey, you should investigate." A company 10 years old, I'm sure you probably don't have any technical debt or anything like that, right?
Dana Lawson:
No, not at all. The world's most largest, I'm inventing that, most largest Mongo cluster that keeps me up at night. No, no, not at all. We did everything right. No, I mean, when you build fast, you're going to have some technical debt. We're always, again, looking at ways to mitigate it. And I have monoliths. Yeah, I know. They say one monolith to rule them all. No, I have multiple monoliths. Thank you very much. As all the other-
Jody Bailey:
Aren't those services then?
Dana Lawson:
Aren't those services? I know. Wasn't microservices supposed to save us? Now I just don't know where anything is. No, it's constantly evolving, constantly not hopefully growing and outgrowing my growth. But I think with reliability, security and anything, you're going to build fast, you're going to learn, you're going to come back, remediate, and go. I think it's a waste of time to go break down monoliths. I think that you have to be smart and build towards the future. That's the best way to approach stuff because I mean, those sins of the past, you probably aren't going to get rid of them. You're just not. And the lifespan of most people at companies, with the exception of some long-lived big companies, just three to four years people make their rotation. And so every three to four years, you're like, "Okay, what technical debt is coming, going, staying?" And you can do your best as a leader, I think, to try and, again, have a written culture and document it.
But yeah, I don't know. People that say they don't have any, they're lying or they aren't big enough yet to accumulate it, but I don't know.
Jody Bailey:
They just started.
Dana Lawson:
Maybe one of these hot new AI companies come teach me something like how to build without building BS. And I'm going to tell you, if they're letting agents do it, a lot of it's going to be crap. So I don't-
Jody Bailey:
Talk to them in 10 years, right?
Dana Lawson:
Talk to me in 10 years. Yeah. But y'all been around forever too. You're one of the OGs. It comes with building something people love. You scale up, but I feel really fortunate. I got a lot of smart people that I work with. We have a pretty darn good track record. The internet would be yelling at us if we were down. So we're always balancing in that product work with that technical debt and that scalability work that you just have to as an engineer.
Jody Bailey:
Yeah, I love that. So it seems pretty apparent that you love what you do. And I'm curious, what drew you to leadership and what advice might you give to others that are thinking about moving into leadership?
Dana Lawson:
Man, I don't know what drew me. I think I'm just mouthing and they're like, "Listen, you're telling everybody what to do anyway. You might as well just be that person." And I was like, "Okay, I stupidly fill up." Honestly, I just love people. I love getting to learn from people. I think the best leaders are the ones that really know that life is better when you put all the best pieces that humans can bring together, because none of us can solve anything independently.
I mean, there's some smarties out there, but they still have their struggles. And for me, I've always just gravitated towards solving all problems with people that are like-minded in the sense that they want to solve them too, and that sense of purpose. And I think for people that are looking to get into this side of a business, which is really helping unlock people and teams, you got to love people, you got to love all the quirks, you got to sounder, you got to think about it and be curious because you know they're going to bring something unique to you because if you're a global team building for a global population, you should try to represent all those weirdos out there.
And so I just try to really stay grounded in the fact that we all have different skills we can leverage and having people feel safe with low ego that we can come and do something magnificent together. Because I do feel like there's a lot of doom and gloom on the TV, but people are doing radical, amazing things, and we can continue to do that if we all come together. And that's what I love about leadership. And if you're doing it for the money or you're doing it because you do think you're going to make all the decisions like, you'll be very unhappy. I mean, you may make some money, but you're going to be miserable and nobody's listening to you. You're selling. I hate to tell you, as a leader, most of your job is selling because you want to be authentic about what you're selling, but you're selling.
Jody Bailey:
Yeah. I love that. Great insights, great feedback. I think Netlify and your team are really lucky to have you and appreciate your time and really enjoyed the conversation. Thank you so much.
Dana Lawson:
Thank you for having me. It's been fun, Jody. I am Dana Lawson, CTO of Netlify. And I'm not on the social webs. I know, how do you survive in tech and not be on X? Well, you come talk to me. No, you can find me on LinkedIn. If you do want to get ahold of me, I accept all connections.
Jody Bailey:
All right. And I'm Jody Bailey and I'm on LinkedIn as well. And like Dana, I'm not really any place else. So we'll see you or hear from you on LinkedIn. Thanks everybody.
Eira May:
That's going to do it for this episode. If you have suggestions for topics to cover or guests to speak with, you can email podcast@stackoverflow.com. My name is Eira May and I've been the B2B editor at Stack Overflow. Today I've been speaking with Jody Bailey, our chief of product and technology at Stack, as well as Dana Lawson, who is CTO at Netlify. Thank you for listening. See you folks on the next episode of Leaders of Code.
```
